<div id="review_1923823847" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1923823847" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1923823847?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781416556961" href="/book/show/2930299-the-lathe-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=fantastic-weird">fantastic-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=new-wave-sf">new-wave-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=near-future">near-future</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=superhuman-powers">superhuman-powers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=magic-realism-reality-as-illusion">magic-realism-reality-as-illusion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=humanistic-sf">humanistic-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1923823847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10331763570041307251"><b>The Lathe of Heaven: An early 1970s classic of reality-altering dreams with Taoist undercurrents</b><br/>Originally posted at <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/the-lathe-of-heaven/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a><br/>I love Ursula K. Le Guin’s novels from the late 1960s and early 70s. She just couldn’t go wrong during this period. Although The Lathe of Heaven may not be the first book that comes to mind as one of her masterpieces (that honor would likely go to The Left Hand of Darkness, The Dispossessed, or the EARTHSEA TRILOGY), it was nominated for the Hugo and Nebula Award</span>
  <span id="freeText10331763570041307251" style="display:none"><b>The Lathe of Heaven: An early 1970s classic of reality-altering dreams with Taoist undercurrents</b><br />Originally posted at <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/the-lathe-of-heaven/" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a><br />I love Ursula K. Le Guin’s novels from the late 1960s and early 70s. She just couldn’t go wrong during this period. Although The Lathe of Heaven may not be the first book that comes to mind as one of her masterpieces (that honor would likely go to The Left Hand of Darkness, The Dispossessed, or the EARTHSEA TRILOGY), it was nominated for the Hugo and Nebula Awards and won the Locus Award in 1972. It’s what I consider one of her smaller books, but still one of her best.<br /><br />What makes The Lathe of Heaven great is that it can tackle some of the biggest issues of the time — overpopulation, environmental destruction, war, racism, the lost soul of the modern world, exploration of the dreaming mind, alternate realities, and the urge to shape society for the better — all in under 200 pages. I really feel that is a lost art in this day of massive doorstoppers, multi-book mega-series, and self-indulgent info-dumps.<br /><br />The story is also simple in concept, with a very small cast of characters, so it could easily be a stage play and has been made into a film twice, once as a PBS production in 1980 and later as an A&amp;E Network film in 2002. It centers on George Orr, an unremarkable man who happens to have “effective” dreams which alter reality. Horrified by this, he tries to suppress his dreams with drugs, but runs afoul of the law and is given the choice between therapy or a mental asylum. He chooses therapy, and is assigned Dr. William Haber.<br /><br />The early parts of the story detail the therapy sessions of George and Dr. Haber. George is a very passive, almost timid man. He doesn’t want to be in this situation, and certainly doesn’t want to be altering reality with his unconscious dreams. Dr. Haber is the polar opposite, a confident, brash, and aggressive man who quickly recognizes the potential to harness George’s dreams to shape reality in the ways he wants. <br /><br />Although he makes repeated and valid arguments as to why he should utilize this unique ability to do good and improve society and the world, each time he inserts suggestions to George such as “let’s imagine a world without overpopulation, war, pollution, racism, etc.,” the outcomes invariably are not what he expected and include some serious unforeseen side-effects. Notably, with each new iteration, Dr. Haber’s status and career seem to also improve.<br /><br />The middle portion of The Lathe of Heaven then explores a serious of alternate realities dreamed up by George’s unconscious with prompting from Dr. Haber. The ways in which things go wrong are quite ingenious, and it’s clear that Le Guin does not subscribe to the power fantasy that someone with the means has the right to shape society and reality to their liking without consultation, even with the best of intentions. As the worlds get stranger and more distorted, Dr. Haber hatches an idea that if he can replicate the process on himself, he can cut the reluctant George out of the equation and dream the world himself exactly to his specifications. This forms the climactic final events of the story.<br /><br /> What adds interest to The Lathe of Heaven and places it firmly in the late 60s &amp; early 70s is not just the political issues of the time, but also the underlying elements of Eastern philosophy, specifically the Taoist quotes at the beginnings of chapters from Chuang Tzu, as well as Tao Te Ching, The Book of the Way and Its Virtue by Lao Tzu, along with western philosophers such as H.G. Wells, Victor Hugo, and even Lafcadio Hearn. You can see how well-read Le Guin is and how much Eastern philosophy was gaining prominence and popularity in the West as an alternative to traditional Western philosophy, especially on college campuses and in intellectual circles. This is similar to the profound influence of the I Ching, The Book of Changes, in Philip K. Dick’s dystopian masterpiece of alternate reality, The Man in the High Castle.<br /><br />Taoist thinking can be found in the character of George. From many perspectives, this protagonist is very frustrating due to his passivity, reluctance to take any action to change the world around him, and instinctual distrust of authoritarian behavior. Whereas some people might seek to harness their powers to shape reality through dreams, George is repelled by this. Taoism is one of those slippery, non-dogmatic philosophies that espouses the pursuit of The Way though natural, uncontrived living. Disciples seek to discard the ills of civilization and material desires and pursue the simple, unadorned joys of a basic agrarian existence. One key concept is called Wu Wei, which is defined as “effortless action,” “non-action,” much as the planets orbit the sun without any effort, just following the natural rhythms of the universe.<br /><br />So while from a Western perspective George is a spineless man, afraid and reluctant to do anything with his powers of dreaming, from a Taoist perspective he might be a very dedicated individual trying to avoid doing harm to the natural order of the world around him. Of course this becomes an interesting point of debate in the story — if Taoists look to the ancient past of a simple existence as the ideal, does this principle still apply in the dystopian future society of George and Dr. Haber, living in massive towers packed with millions of people living on minimal rations due to overpopulation, a deteriorating environment, wars throughout Europe and the Middle East, and a general spiritual malaise? Faced with such conditions, is it wrong for Dr. Haber to want to change that? And is it right for George to resist any such manipulations? As always, it is the questions that Le Guin raises that are more important than the answers. The Lathe of Heaven is a concise, though-provoking journey into multiple realities and the dreaming unconscious, but is in no way an escape from reality.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10331763570041307251" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_459135141" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/459135141" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3073544-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472052100p2/3073544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/459135141?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3073544-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781416556961" href="/book/show/2930299-the-lathe-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer459135141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16289317808298434615">Coincidentally I had just previously read (part of) <i>Ubik</i> by Philip K. Dick which is <i>also</i> a novel about a person 'gifted' with the power to change the past retroactively, so my opinion of <i>The Lathe of Heaven</i> was probably (unfairly) affected by this glut—do two books qualify as a <i>glut</i>?—of past-altering fiction in my reading schedule. I want to alter the past and start with a different Ursula K. Le Guin novel instead. <br/><br/>As a disclaimer of sorts, I have to admit that these kind of wackadoo premises ar</span>
  <span id="freeText16289317808298434615" style="display:none">Coincidentally I had just previously read (part of) <i>Ubik</i> by Philip K. Dick which is <i>also</i> a novel about a person 'gifted' with the power to change the past retroactively, so my opinion of <i>The Lathe of Heaven</i> was probably (unfairly) affected by this glut—do two books qualify as a <i>glut</i>?—of past-altering fiction in my reading schedule. I want to alter the past and start with a different Ursula K. Le Guin novel instead. <br /><br />As a disclaimer of sorts, I have to admit that these kind of wackadoo premises are a tough sell for me. It's not that I'm an intensely rational reader who expects rigorous scientific realism from all of my literary entertainments; it's just that there are certain things I'm willing to suspend my disbelief for—ghosts, aliens, talking dogs—and other things that seem too metaphysically muddled to accept—like the subjective manipulation of the past and Adam Sandler's film career—just to name a few.<br /><br />I thought Le Guin would overcome the raising of my eyebrow. She's certainly no slouch in the writing department and leaves many of her genre colleagues in the dust, but the plot gets old after awhile. This milquetoast guy named George Orr discovers that his dreams have the power to change reality (so that nobody else in the world notices the difference), so he starts abusing drugs to stave off sleep. A psychiatrist named Dr. Haber is at first skeptical about George's talents but later discovers he can manipulate them and turn himself into a makeshift god. What follows are too many episodes of Haber trying to 'save' the world (often with negative results) by directing George's dreams—while George frets about the rightness or wrongness of the arrangement with little consequence.<br /><br />You can almost hear the simple construction of the tale buckling and snapping under the weight of the ponderous allegory. For the first two-thirds, <i>The Lathe of Heaven</i> was a diverting little narrative, but at the end it got to be too much and too little at the same time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16289317808298434615" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_55253406" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/55253406" />
    <a title="tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/230113-tim"><img alt="tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458012692p2/230113.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/55253406?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="tim" href="/user/show/230113-tim">tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781416556961" href="/book/show/2930299-the-lathe-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer55253406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13422903265917156038">I've always assumed chronic readers share the experience of finding connecting patterns from one book to the next. No matter how seemingly disparate books read consecutively may be, I've always come across overlapping concepts or some sort of shared meaning that is more difficult to pin down and describe. Whatever these synchronicities may be, I am always genuinely amazed and interpret them as signs that I'm witnessing something important--or at the very least, that I am reading the right book a</span>
  <span id="freeText13422903265917156038" style="display:none">I've always assumed chronic readers share the experience of finding connecting patterns from one book to the next. No matter how seemingly disparate books read consecutively may be, I've always come across overlapping concepts or some sort of shared meaning that is more difficult to pin down and describe. Whatever these synchronicities may be, I am always genuinely amazed and interpret them as signs that I'm witnessing something important--or at the very least, that I am reading the right book at the right time. <br /><br />In the last several books I've read have been an uncanny amount of synchronicities bouncing off each other as I made my way through them. I won't bore with details, but the momentum of these emerging, overlapping patterns seemed to coalesce and bleed over into "real" life yesterday after just finishing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59924.The_Lathe_of_Heaven" title="The Lathe of Heaven by Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lathe of Heaven</a>. It's not the first time this has happened, nor the most remarkable occurrence, but worth sharing nonetheless.<br /><br />Ursula K. Le Guin has existed in the periphery of my awareness for some time, but until now I hadn't read her. Upon finishing the book I read before this one, I had no idea what I would read next. My preference upon finishing a book is not to premeditate what I should read next, but instead let the "choice" come to me. So it was that when I finished <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1060184.Schrodinger_s_Cat_Trilogy__The_Universe_Next_Door___The_Trick_Top_Hat___The_Homing_Pigeons_" title="Schrodinger's Cat Trilogy (The Universe Next Door / The Trick Top Hat / The Homing Pigeons) by Robert Anton Wilson" rel="nofollow noopener">Schrodinger's Cat Trilogy The Universe Next Door The Trick Top Hat The Homing Pigeons</a> that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59924.The_Lathe_of_Heaven" title="The Lathe of Heaven by Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lathe of Heaven</a> popped into my head. I checked the library, a copy happened to be available right then, and so it was. Finally I should read one of the so-called required reads for all Portlanders. <br /><br />I'm not normally taken to do what is suggested of me, but in this case it worked out. Not only did I really enjoy the journey, but I could only laugh when I discovered its influence did not end on the last page. <br /><br />So yesterday, upon reading the last page, my family and I decided to catch a bus to Cathedral Park under the St. Johns Bridge--featured in the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59924.The_Lathe_of_Heaven" title="The Lathe of Heaven by Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lathe of Heaven</a>--and take in some free music. Okay, no, that is not the profound connection. It couldn't be, as that "choice" was determined by us. What wasn't determined by us was that on our way home we accidentally hopped on the wrong bus. It was hot and humid, we weren't thinking right, and the air conditioned bus looked only too inviting. Besides, the bus would eventually loop around and head back downtown. Anyway, as this bus took us farther and farther away from downtown and out into the boondocks, the bus driver pulled over to take a 40-minute break in the "town" of Linnton. So here we were, with no intention of our own, temporarily stuck in the roadside dilapidation of an old settlement 15 miles northwest of Portland. Pinned between industrial sprawl, railroads, the Willamette River, and the gorgeous green hills of Forest Park, it is a surreal landscape to say the least. More to the point, Linnton is where the second to last imagined scene takes place in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59924.The_Lathe_of_Heaven" title="The Lathe of Heaven by Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lathe of Heaven</a>.<br /><br />I've lived in Portland nearly 15 years and had never before stepped foot in Linnton. Why would I? Yet, mere hours after encountering Linnton in the finishing pages of a novel, here I was, unplanned for, but really there. Maybe that's not so strange after all, but when the literature I read begins to flow over into and influence the life I lead outside of books, I take notice. <br /><br />That's it. That's my story. I'm sorry if I built this up into a major let down. But it <i>felt</i> major to me at the time.<br /><br />Oh, and regarding the book itself: I initially thought the character development lacked dimension until I realized this novel is not so much about character development as it is about the seriousness of possible ecological catastrophe and the unknown healing power and potential of dreaming. Read it. It may influence <i>your</i> "real" life too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13422903265917156038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_160592568" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160592568" />
    <a title="Meredith Holley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1302775-meredith-holley"><img alt="Meredith Holley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548641865p2/1302775.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/160592568?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meredith Holley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meredith Holley" href="/user/show/1302775-meredith-holley">Meredith Holley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">C.S. Lewis fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Meredith by: </span>
      Ceridwen
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1302775-meredith-holley?shelf=utopia-dystopia">utopia-dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1302775-meredith-holley?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1302775-meredith-holley?shelf=beards-are-mesmerizing">beards-are-mesmerizing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1302775-meredith-holley?shelf=pacific-northwest-glory">pacific-northwest-glory</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1302775-meredith-holley?shelf=slaves">slaves</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1302775-meredith-holley?shelf=up-hill-both-ways-in-the-snow">up-hill-both-ways-in-the-snow</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer160592568" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer254458821315464825">I have long been a fan of dreams: talking about dreams, working out the interweavings between dreaming life and reality. I almost scare-quoted reality there, but then I realized that this review is probably going to be douchey enough as it is without adding a scare-quoted reality to it. Anyway, Ursula LeGuin’s worlds are typically not my worlds; when I’m reading her books, I tend to bump into walls and trip over furniture, where other readers intuitively know the lay of the interior decorating. </span>
  <span id="freeText254458821315464825" style="display:none">I have long been a fan of dreams: talking about dreams, working out the interweavings between dreaming life and reality. I almost scare-quoted reality there, but then I realized that this review is probably going to be douchey enough as it is without adding a scare-quoted reality to it. Anyway, Ursula LeGuin’s worlds are typically not my worlds; when I’m reading her books, I tend to bump into walls and trip over furniture, where other readers intuitively know the lay of the interior decorating. And, that is just the way reading goes, I think. Neither bad nor good. Sometimes an author puts the couch were we would like to sit, and other times not. This book, though. This is the LeGuin for me. This book is lovely in a way I can understand.<br /><br />I grew up in a sometimes-fundamentalist home, so for those who didn’t, this comparison might sound like an insult. Please know that I don’t mean it that way. It strikes me that in some pretty superficial ways, <i>The Lathe of Heaven</i> is to Daoism what <i>Narnia</i> is to Christianity. In making that comparison, I am really comparing two things I love, even though they are both representing two very different value systems. I think that both present an emotionally symbolic world in which the roots of a belief system can grow in a simple and understandable way. I think both do a really good job of not sacrificing story to allegory, but still forming a perceptible spiritual message. <br /><br />The other preliminary thought I have is a spoiler about Heather, so I’ll hide it. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[It strikes me as really interesting that Heather starts out as a femme fatale, swings over to being a domestic goddess, and then winds up somewhere in the middle. I don’t have anything profound to say about that, but it did make me think about how any woman can play those parts and it would not be informative of who she really is. It made me think about how it is easy to adopt caricatures and difficult to know who we really are. It is easy to play a role, but difficult to be human. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />But, that is really only about the structure of <i>Lathe</i>, and what I really want to talk about is dreams. In <i>Lathe</i>, George Orr has “effective” dreams that change his reality. That is the basic premise that you find out at the opening of the story, and I will try not to spoil the plot beyond that. Joel was making the point that the story is a reflection on writing, which I think is an interesting, but narrow, reading of the story, and honestly was not how the story resonated with me at all. I think it is a good point, though, and worth noting. A writer re-creates the world, and in that way probably also shapes other people’s perceptions of the world. I think in many ways, though, we all do that, writing or no writing.<br /><br />I guess the way the story resonated with me was more literal than Joel’s reading. I do think that any of us can have a dream in the Martin Luther King, Jr., sense, and that dream can guide culture, but I also think that literal dreams can do that, and maybe that is more where the book fascinated me. In college, I once went to sleep with no interest in a boy in my class and I woke up with a crush on him that it took me months to get over. And all that happened was that, in a dream I had that night, he looked at me a certain way. Dreams seem mysterious and mysteriously powerful to me. I had a dream like that this week, and the content of it is not very important, but there was a snake in it, and the snake was also human, and the dream changed something to me, so I thought of this book. I’m not sure what it changed, but it was just different than other dreams.<br /><br />Once, in college, my best friend from high school had a dream in which we were both preparing for her wedding. About a year later, I had the same dream but from my point of view, which I didn’t realized until later that night I started describing the dream to her and she knew all of its details before I told her, but from her own point of view. <br /><br />In my part of the dream, after she got married, I went to help an ex-boyfriend move his things into a new house and there was a soundtrack in that part, which is something I don't think I've had in another dream. After I woke up, I was walking to work and I put the Velvet Underground <i>Loaded</i> CD into my discman (I had bought it the day before). “Who Loves the Sun” came on, I realized it was the song in my dream, and I looked up and saw my ex-boyfriend sitting in front of the house he had moved into in my dream. The whole day was off, with the people I cared about in my dreaming and waking life crossing over.<br /><br />I don’t have a moral or a lesson to that story, but it was an experience I had that made me wonder whether my dreams were creeping in to my reality, like they do with poor George Orr. And I do think many dreams can shape the world in a way I don’t understand, in a way that makes me small and brittle. I think LeGuin captures that literal power of dreams very gracefully, without creating a heavy-handed allegory, leaving room for many applications of the tone and texture of the story. I also love what she does with George and his therapist, and the yin and yang of their personalities, though I can't think of more to say about that than just stating it. I’m glad I found a LeGuin that is for me; I’m glad somebody wrote a story about dreams.</span>
  <a data-text-id="254458821315464825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12377418021055787275">This book was a bit strange and has given me strange dreams for several nights in a row. It's making me a little paranoid haha! I'm not sure I cared so much for how the story was told, as the conversations between the character of George Orr and Dr. Haber were a mixture of confusing/boring to me.   <span id="freeText12377418021055787275" style="display:none">This book was a bit strange and has given me strange dreams for several nights in a row. It's making me a little paranoid haha! I'm not sure I cared so much for how the story was told, as the conversations between the character of George Orr and Dr. Haber were a mixture of confusing/boring to me. However, I did enjoy how much the book made me think about things - how changing the past for everyone could make things better/worse, how some things on a global scale cannot be overcome (peace, racial differences, sickness), and how too much power in any one person can be catastrophic no matter how good their intentions may be. I'm sad that the author passed away this year but I will definitely pick up other books from her, as this was sadly my first.<br /><br />As a side note, this book was published in 1971 and though it was set in the future with a bunch of advanced technology, I found it kind of amusing when the character at one point looks up an office in a phone book, a thing solidly of the past now. It makes me wonder what things I regularly use now that will soon be extinct.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3465058264" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3465058264" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3465058264?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22007410-the-lathe-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3465058264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4939398109187269548">[9/10]<br/><br/><b> <i> “Speech is silver, silence is gold. Self is universe. Please forgive interruption, crossing in mist.” </i> </b><br/><br/>First published in 1971, “The Lathe of Heaven” is using a science-fiction framework for a thought-provoking dialogue about the individual’s place in the world, about the clash of two radically different philosophies of life. Contemplative Oriental schools of thought, so popular in the Flower Power period, meet Western positive action and will-to-power. The title itself, despite its orig</span>
  <span id="freeText4939398109187269548" style="display:none">[9/10]<br /><br /><b> <i> “Speech is silver, silence is gold. Self is universe. Please forgive interruption, crossing in mist.” </i> </b><br /><br />First published in 1971, “The Lathe of Heaven” is using a science-fiction framework for a thought-provoking dialogue about the individual’s place in the world, about the clash of two radically different philosophies of life. Contemplative Oriental schools of thought, so popular in the Flower Power period, meet Western positive action and will-to-power. The title itself, despite its original mistranslation of ‘scourge’ as ‘lathe’ comes from ancient Chinese texts describing The Way to enlightenment. [it could also be a case of the old saying that ‘the path to hell is paved with good intentions’]<br /><br />Most of the story revolves around meetings in a psychiatry cabinet between an ordinary man from Portland and his court-appointed doctor. George Orr, pleading for the contemplative school, is afraid to sleep, since his dreams are different from everybody else. Orr has the power to affect reality with his dreams, and is the only person aware of the changes in the world when he eventually wakes up. <br /><br /><i> He's a real nowhere man<br />Sitting in his nowhere land<br />Making all his nowhere plans for nobody<br /><br />Doesn't have a point of view<br />Knows not where he's going to<br />Isn't he a bit like you and me?<br />Nowhere man please listen<br />You don't know what you're missing<br />Nowhere man, the world is at your command </i> [The Beatles, 1970]<br /><br />Dr. William Haber, an ordinary practitioner, is quickly realizing the incredible power of George Orr’s talent, and sets out to profit from guiding his patient’s dreams, under deep hypnosis, in the direction he thinks would best benefit the world at large (with personal perks as a bonus).<br /><br />The future world presented in the novel is clearly dystopian, and could do with some improvement. Current trends from the seventies are exacerbated: overpopulation, militarism, government sponsored surveillance, global warming, pollution, income inequality. But what right has one man to decide what is best for the world? And who will be responsible for the unforeseen consequences when the targeted solution turns out to be worse than the status-quo?<br /><br /><b> “You can’t go on changing things, trying to run things.”<br />“You speak as if that were some kind of general moral imperative.” [...]<br />“It’s wrong to change the pattern of things. It won’t do. It’s been our mistake for a hundred years.” </b><br /><br />Science-fiction has always been concerned with general moral imperatives, with the direction humanity and our planet are heading into. Reaching for the stars or down a fast road to Hell? For George Orr and William Haber the future of mankind is played out in these weekly sleep therapy sessions.<br /><br /><b> The dream is the aquarium of night </b> [Victor Hugo]<br /><br />The first chapters are a very informative, rigorously researched and well presented study of sleep, this activity that covers about a third of our span on Earth. I might have some personal reservations about the practice and stated results of hypnosis, but for the rest I was captivated by descriptions of the different stages of sleep, by the methods of study available at the time and even by the glimpses of the biochemistry taking place in the synapses. Pretty soon it became obvious though that the focus of the novel is not the study of sleep, but the search for a solution to the destructive trends already mentioned, and still a major concern in 2020 : war, pollution, global warming, overpopulation, police-state, etc. Because science-fiction strives to do a little more than just pointing out the problems: it searches for alternative paths.<br /><br /><b> The quality of the will to power is, precisely, growth. Achievement is its cancellation. To be, the will to power must increase with each fulfillment, making the fulfillment only a step to a further one. The vaster the power, the vaster the appetite for more. </b><br /><br />So, how do we stop the likes of William Haber from becoming the evil masterminds of the universe? Le Guin, like many others of her generation, turned her focus towards ancient Oriental wisdom.<br /><br /><i> It was more than dignity. Integrity? Wholeness? Like a block of wood not carved.<br />The infinite possibility, the unlimited and unqualified wholeness of being of the uncommitted, the non-acting, the uncarved: the being who, being nothing but himself, is everything. </i><br /><br />As a sidenote, this Western/Eastern philosophical duality, contemplation versus will-to-power, is exactly what David Mitchell will do several decades later with “Cloud Atlas”. Le Guin has the merit of going there beforehand and of being much more elegant and concise than the sprawling and, at times, self-indulgent modern saga.<br /><br />As a second sidenote, I keep talking about Eastern philosophies, about Confucius or Buddha, yin /yang, passive resistance, conqueror versus gardener, etc. but if one goes far back enough in our own European heritage we come across Platonic dialogues as a format for debate and a plea for balance in everything we do from Socrates.<br /><br /><b> Neither dominant nor submissive. Independence / dependence – same thing. Creative / destructive, on the Ramirez scale – same thing. Both, neither. Either, or. Where there’s an opposed pair, a polarity, you’re in the middle; where there’s a scale, you’re at the balance point. </b><br /><br />George Orr represents the only hope we have as a race. He is a nowhere man, but he is steadfast in his moral compass (without the crutch of an organized religion I might add). His story is proof that every life matters, that the world is not a given entity outside our control, but a work in progress shaped by every individual action or inaction. The future is our personal responsibility, and we cannot give up the burden when things go tough, raise our collective shoulders and say, ‘that’s the way the cookie crumbles’. We must live according to those <i> general moral imperatives </i> when common sense and common decency tell us we are part of a community going forward and not lonely souls covering in terror in a dark basement.<br /><br /><b> Are there really people without resentment, without hate? she wondered. People who never go cross-grained to the universe? Who recognize evil, and resist evil, and yet are utterly unaffected by it?<br />Of course there are. Countless, the living and the dead. Those who have returned in pure compassion to the wheel, those who follow the way that cannot be followed without knowing they follow it, [...] There are enough of them, enough to keep us going. Perhaps. </b><br /><br />If we’re really lucky, we are blessed with companions along the way, people who trust us and love us for who we are and not for what they can get from us. George Orr has Heather, a lawyer who is invited to witness a sleep therapy session and stays to get know Orr better and to find out what he stands for.<br /><br /><i> Love doesn’t just sit there, like a stone, it has to be made, like bread; remade all the time, made new. </i><br /><br />Their relationship follows along the escalation of the conflict between the desire to rule the world from Haber, and the passive resistance of Orr. It’s a bumpy road, but who would be such a Grinch to deny them this small compensation in an otherwise bleak dystopian landscape?<br /><br />cue another Beatles song from the 1970 album : “The Long and Winding Road”<br /><br />Finally, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ as aliens arrive to save us from ourselves, in a fashion similar to classic Kurt Vonnegut stories  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Orr and Haber and Heather have their spectacular showdown where of course the fate of reality hangs in the balance as dreams become nightmares, and wisdom is shown to rely on the heart, and not on technology.<br /><br /><b> We’re in the world, not against it. It doesn’t work to try stand outside things and run them that way. It just doesn’t work, it goes against life. There is a way but you have to follow it. The world is, no matter how we think it ought to be. You have to be with it. You have to let it be. </b><br /><br />cue the final 1970 Beatles track in the background with “Let It Be!”<br /><br />Curtain!<br /><br />Applause!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4939398109187269548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_176646288" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176646288" />
    <a title="Catie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1886642-catie"><img alt="Catie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385169623p2/1886642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176646288?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catie" href="/user/show/1886642-catie">Catie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Catie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana">Tatiana</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176646288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17580353427800140377">I think that the contents of these deceptively meager one hundred and seventy five pages could keep my neurons firing for months. This is a tiny little volume, which I thought I would have read within a few hours. But I drastically underestimated the amount of time that I would need to sit and process certain pages, or to have inner debates about certain scenes, and I <i>really</i> didn’t anticipate the sheer number of dog-eared pages I would end up with at the close of this volume. This book is a mind</span>
  <span id="freeText17580353427800140377" style="display:none">I think that the contents of these deceptively meager one hundred and seventy five pages could keep my neurons firing for months. This is a tiny little volume, which I thought I would have read within a few hours. But I drastically underestimated the amount of time that I would need to sit and process certain pages, or to have inner debates about certain scenes, and I <i>really</i> didn’t anticipate the sheer number of dog-eared pages I would end up with at the close of this volume. This book is a mind-stretching, profound, gorgeously written masterpiece.<br /><br />At age 16, George Orr realizes with horror that his dreams have the power to change the reality that he lives in. When he dreams “effectively,” reality is shifted to a new timeline, wherein the contents of his dream are and forever have been real. He lives with this ability for over a decade, until he is filled with anxiety and desperate to prevent these dreams. He is a passive man, and he does not want so much power. He eventually finds himself in the care of Dr. William Haber, a psychiatrist who slowly begins to realize the magnitude of George’s ability. But Dr. Haber is not a passive man. He is the antithesis: a proactive, aggressive, driven, and curious man, determined to change the world for the better. Dr. Haber believes that George’s ability can be channeled and compelled to improve the world, with himself at the helm. George begins to struggle against his own passivity, as he witnesses the changes wrought by his manipulated dreams. <i>”The end justifies the means. But what if there never is an end? All we have is means.”</i><br /><br />The writing is vividly descriptive and I often felt enfolded by the images of the changing world and George’s haunting dreams. I love this passage, which describes an explosion:<br /><br /><i>”The stars were coming out, white as daisies. A big one was blooming just to the right of the road, low over the dark country, tremulously white. When he looked up at it again it had already become larger and brighter. </i>It’s enhuging<i>, he thought. It seemed to grow reddish as it brightened. It enreddenhuged. The eyes swam. Small blue-green streaks zipped about it zigzagging Brownian roundianroundian. A vast and creamy halo pulsated about big star and tiny zips, fainter, clearer, pulsing.</i> Oh no oh no!<i> he said as the big star brightened hugendly BURST blinding.”</i><br /><br />For me, the most interesting question posed by this book is, what is the role of humans on this planet? Are we somehow set above and apart of this world or are we one unremarkable animal species among many? Do we have a duty and prerogative to change the world, to make things better? Can we? Are we capable of positive change, or are we like overgrown brutal children with too much power and not enough perception and wisdom to use it in a positive way? <br /><br />What of those very few whose decisions and actions affect real, sweeping change on the world? Can one person alone be trusted with that sort of power? How do we decide who gets to wield it? What of those who have power forced upon them, but never desired it? Would it be a burden or a responsibility? Could it become an addiction? <br /><br />There’s a minor love story here, but I don’t agree that this book is all about “love conquering all.” I was actually a bit bored by the whole romance, until she threw this little sparkly at me: <i>”Love doesn’t just sit there, like a stone, it has to be made, like bread; re-made all the time, made new”</i> which basically sums up my entire philosophy about marriage in one sentence. <br /><br />But I still don’t think that this book is about love. I think that this book is about faith. And no, I don’t mean capital F Faith. I mean faith: the ability to float, to give yourself over to living, and to trust that you will be carried in the right direction; that you will be cared for. I mean the faith to let yourself move along as a very small part of a whole; to be at peace within because this world will never be at peace; to trust that you can let the person that you love the most slip through your fingers, and then find her again in one form or another. <br /> <br /><u>Perfect Musical Pairing</u><br /><br />Beethoven – <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=CC26f1XBKjQ" rel="nofollow noopener">String Quartet in B-flat major, Cavatina</a><br /><br />One masterpiece deserves another. This is one of my favorite classical pieces, and one of my favorite pieces of music period. To me this music is all about the inevitability of suffering and pain, and finding the means within to move with grace through life and accept the joys whenever they come. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17580353427800140377" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6485096?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/384057-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">science fiction fans, philosophers, psychologists, international volunteers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384057-alex?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/384057-alex?shelf=sff">sff</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6485096" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11543282630500842147">The Lathe Of Heaven is a taoist parable masquerading as a novella. Through the metaphor of George Orr, a man whose dreams become reality, it examines the consequences of interference and the hubris of believing that we can "improve" the world.<br/><br/>I read this book during a flight to Central America, where I was going to spend the summer before my second year of medical school doing HIV/AIDS education. The contrast could not have been more striking: the purpose of my summer and my career was to interf</span>
  <span id="freeText11543282630500842147" style="display:none">The Lathe Of Heaven is a taoist parable masquerading as a novella. Through the metaphor of George Orr, a man whose dreams become reality, it examines the consequences of interference and the hubris of believing that we can "improve" the world.<br /><br />I read this book during a flight to Central America, where I was going to spend the summer before my second year of medical school doing HIV/AIDS education. The contrast could not have been more striking: the purpose of my summer and my career was to interfere, to improve, to attempt to mitigate some of the consequences of poverty, ignorance, and social (mis)planning in the US and the world. The Lathe of Heaven made me look long and hard at my motives and, while it did not persuade me that I was wrong in my desire to help, it planted a seed of doubt to counter the inevitable self-righteousness of the volunteer. I think this book should be mandatory reading for everyone who wants to "make the world a better place." That is a noble sentiment, but it's worth a warning that sometimes we may get what we wish for.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11543282630500842147" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2128144890" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2128144890" />
    <a title="Dave" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35045983-dave"><img alt="Dave" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496413985p2/35045983.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2128144890?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dave" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dave" href="/user/show/35045983-dave">Dave</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22007410-the-lathe-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35045983-dave?shelf=science-fiction-classic">science-fiction-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35045983-dave?shelf=science-fiction-all">science-fiction-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35045983-dave?shelf=read-borrowed">read-borrowed</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2128144890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10980711298944904576">Le Guin’s “The Lathe of Heaven” first came to my attention through a low budget PBS film 🎞 of the same title, released on tv in 1980. A science fiction story with aliens, but so hauntingly unique that it literally gave me nightmares. And, that’s appropriate because the tool that Le Guin used to explore her different themes here was dreams, specifically dreams that alter reality. Because when George Orr dreams, reality is transformed both in the past and the present and its as if the previous rea</span>
  <span id="freeText10980711298944904576" style="display:none">Le Guin’s “The Lathe of Heaven” first came to my attention through a low budget PBS film 🎞 of the same title, released on tv in 1980. A science fiction story with aliens, but so hauntingly unique that it literally gave me nightmares. And, that’s appropriate because the tool that Le Guin used to explore her different themes here was dreams, specifically dreams that alter reality. Because when George Orr dreams, reality is transformed both in the past and the present and its as if the previous reality never existed. But it’s scary for George and he doesn’t want to fall asleep 💤 so he takes unauthorized drugs to dull his senses and keep him awake. <br /><br />George is a simple man, a decent man. He doesn’t want to hurt anyone or blink anyone out of existence. And perhaps he’s too simple because he is forced to go to a psychiatrist instead of pleading guilty to the unauthorized drugs and Dr.Haber is no simpleton. He sees power and is determined to wield it for the betterment of the human race. Like Galadriel 🧝‍♀️ and the One Ring (see LOTR), George and Dr. Haber have to pass the test. Can they give up absolute power or let it consume them? <br /><br />So, it’s an allegory about do-gooders who want to make the world a better place through systems of control and power, like Communism. Be careful what you wish for because it may not end up how you expect. Your utopia may be a nightmare. If you wish to stop overpopulation in the teeming cities, you could end up with a plague and billions who were never born. Think back to the genie and the three wishes. How does that always turn out.<br /><br />And, is it fair for one or more enlightened people to dictate how the world will work for everyone else. <br /><br />The other interesting thing Le Guin does is she takes issues such as have been explored with time travel 🧳 and does them one better by positing an everchanging reality that Dr. Haber controls through suggestions to George’s effective dreams. What is real and what isn’t? <br /><br />An evocative, beautiful story that explores so many 💡 ideas.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10980711298944904576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_70493497" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/70493497" />
    <a title="Robert" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1433865-robert"><img alt="Robert" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1254324661p2/1433865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/70493497?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert" href="/user/show/1433865-robert">Robert</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780380013203" href="/book/show/883266.The_Lathe_of_Heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1433865-robert?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1433865-robert?shelf=sf">sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70493497" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10177610331294443398">I always say my favourite film is Raiders of the Lost Ark and my favourite book is the original Earthsea trilogy, or if pushed A Wizard of Earthsea. Picking one favourite is always a bit arbitrary but both of these are childhood favourites that have survived repeat viewings/readings and have developed accretions of personal associations that add to their significance to me. I think I can now go a step further and say that LeGuin is becoming my favourite author because she just has so many comple</span>
  <span id="freeText10177610331294443398" style="display:none">I always say my favourite film is Raiders of the Lost Ark and my favourite book is the original Earthsea trilogy, or if pushed A Wizard of Earthsea. Picking one favourite is always a bit arbitrary but both of these are childhood favourites that have survived repeat viewings/readings and have developed accretions of personal associations that add to their significance to me. I think I can now go a step further and say that LeGuin is becoming my favourite author because she just has so many completely top-notch books: books that are about something, are thought-provoking, are elegantly written, tell excellently crafted stories about real people (though often in unreal situations) and reward repeat reading. The USA should consider her a National Treasure. <br /><br />The Lathe of Heaven must go on my list of these superb works. It has a main protagonist whose dreams can retro-actively change reality. That's an idea any science-fiction writer would have made a pact with the Devil for - but most of them would have then squandered it on mere pyrotechnics.<br /><br />LeGuin saves the pyrotechnics not for the story, but the story-telling; pyroclastics feature in the story, however. What do I mean by pyrotechnical story telling? Here's an example: A second-rank protagonist gets some brief promotions to view-point status. In the longest of these, she starts the scene in an extremely agitated, anxious state of mind. The prose is all short sentences and sharp edges. As the scene progresses over several pages, she gradually calms down and the prose gradually shifts to the calm, languid prose readers would more usually associate with LeGuin. This use of style to emphasise the mood of the character is clever and successful but is merely in the service of her aims. Too many of her contemporaries would consider such deployment of technique as the <i>purpose</i> of the book itself.<br /><br />So if you haven't read this book, please do so - this is literature at its finest.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10177610331294443398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_583161801" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/583161801" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/695116-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340192527p2/695116.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/583161801?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/695116-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781441745804" href="/book/show/15751546-the-lathe-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=apocalyptic">apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=1audio">1audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=2fiction">2fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=3classics">3classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer583161801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10073013469949767072">My edition is by Blackstone, but was downloaded from the local library. The reader was excellent, but I really would have liked it if they could have put the actual Beatles' tune in.<br/><br/>This review contains overall spoilers, I've only hidden specific ones. It's a 40+ year old book & has had 2 movies based on it, so unless you've been living under a rock [as far as the SF genre goes], you'll probably know most of it.<br/><br/>This is a tough review to write because there are just so many threads running throug</span>
  <span id="freeText10073013469949767072" style="display:none">My edition is by Blackstone, but was downloaded from the local library. The reader was excellent, but I really would have liked it if they could have put the actual Beatles' tune in.<br /><br />This review contains overall spoilers, I've only hidden specific ones. It's a 40+ year old book &amp; has had 2 movies based on it, so unless you've been living under a rock [as far as the SF genre goes], you'll probably know most of it.<br /><br />This is a tough review to write because there are just so many threads running through the book. It's absolutely masterful &amp; strikes me differently each time I read it. I first read it sometime in the 70's, not long after it came out. That was a different time in many ways. Pollution in the cities was at its height, a global nuclear conflict was a real possibility. While we no longer practiced air raid drills by hiding under desks or in hallways, they were still pretty fresh in my mind. A big push was on for ZPG (Zero Population Growth) &amp; environmental activism was really getting going. <br /><br />In the story, every change addresses another theme including, but not limited to eugenics, racial prejudice, diversity, &amp; more. She did this with just a few characters, too. <br /><br />LeGuin got the future both right &amp; wrong, but her mistakes made her points as well or better than her successes. Global warming when scientists were thinking we would create global cooling by air pollution reflecting too much. Her figures for overpopulation were very low. A global population of 7 billion had us all on practically starvation diets while we now have that many &amp; are fat &amp; happy here in the U.S., in large part due to scientific advances &amp; genetic tampering (wheat designed to resist rust). Inflation was a bit of comic relief. A super expensive vacation home was $100K, then an outrageous figure. Now, pretty cheap. <br /><br />Generally, this book is a parable about the unintended consequences, sort of an updated version of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8779896.The_Monkey_s_Paw" title="The Monkey's Paw by W.W. Jacobs" rel="nofollow noopener">The Monkey's Paw</a>, of allowing science &amp; technology fix our ills. Society &amp; science are personified in George Orr, the every man, &amp; Dr. William Haber, the scientist. Orr wants to leave things as they are, except in the most dire of circumstances, while Haber wants to fix the obvious ills (racial prejudice, overpopulation, etc.) of the world. The mechanism for change is Orr's dreams which are controlled by his unreasoning mind, his subconscious. It may seem like an obvious recipe for disaster, but Haber believes he can control the situation &amp; things are so bad that they hardly seem as if they can get worse. <br /><br />By all the tests, Orr is The Average Man, both mentally &amp; physically. He endures &amp; is practically without ambition. He's so easy-going that I want to kick him half the time, yet wind up caring more for him &amp; his plight than Haber. Orr is like a puppy, he can't help his circumstances &amp; brings out the paternal instinct. That same instinct makes me wish he'd grow up &amp; stand up for himself, though. He's perfectly named, too. George Orr immediately brings to mind <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3706.George_Orwell" title="George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">George Orwell</a> &amp; his most famous books championing the every man, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5472.Animal_Farm___1984" title="Animal Farm / 1984 by George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Animal Farm &amp; 1984</a>.<br /><br />Haber is a personable doctor, a shrink, so really does want to help people, but has a huge ego &amp; believes he knows more than he really does - a pert near perfect characterization. His addiction to power &amp; success is understandable. While he's shown in the worst light, I still liked him. He wasn't always wrong &amp; I shared his frustration with Orr.<br /><br />I know the overall story is pretty well spoiled, but the following discusses specifics.<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />I loved how Haber discovered the reality of Orr's power. It was so perfectly staged. A snow capped Mt Hood gets replaced with a true picture which was created from the dream's pile of horse shit. That pretty much spelled out the rest of the story. LOL!<br /><br />The slipperiness of memory was very well done. The way Haber could remember certain things, but could also forget - wishful thinking perfectly portrayed. It made so much sense in the context of the story. It was truly chilling how he blamed Orr for screwing things up when he was personally in danger, but didn't seem upset at all when billions died, even though he lost his entire family.<br /><br />I loved how few characters she had: Orr &amp; Dr. Haber are most of the story, of course. Heather &amp; an alien (they're completely interchangeable, so I count them as 1) to make some points. Manny, the old stoner. He doesn't do much but is kind of fun.<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />There have been 2 movies made from this book. I've seen the 1980 PBS version. It was really good, especially considering its low budget &amp; LeGuin approved of it. I've never seen the 2002 remake &amp; don't intend to since I've heard from too many sources that it changed things &amp; not for the better. <br />Wikipedia has a pretty good write-up here, but be careful of spoilers:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Lathe_of_Heaven">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Lath...</a><br /><br />I can't recommend this book highly enough. It's short, but not necessarily a quick read, especially on a re-read when you can take time to think over some of the points. There are plenty to think over, especially when you think about how we're keeping so many people alive today.<br /><br />I also reviewed the paperback edition here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/54890784">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10073013469949767072" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_434198051" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/434198051" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/434198051?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">solipsistic and uncertain conciousnesses</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nate D by: </span>
      mandated psychotherapy
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=70s-delerium">70s-delerium</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=oneirics">oneirics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer434198051" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2788273171979292396">Thoughtful and terrifying and compulsively entertaining. This is what the science fiction genre was made for.<br/><br/>Taking one of the most drastic hypotheticals, the most direly destabilizing of test cases, LeGuin sets about addressing the ambiguous and subjective nature of reality (in many ways, she out-PKDs her contemporary Phillip K. Dick, here) in direct but subtly calibrated ways, spinning off deep and troubling tangents worthy of their own stories as barest afterthought. Then she uses her sanely-</span>
  <span id="freeText2788273171979292396" style="display:none">Thoughtful and terrifying and compulsively entertaining. This is what the science fiction genre was made for.<br /><br />Taking one of the most drastic hypotheticals, the most direly destabilizing of test cases, LeGuin sets about addressing the ambiguous and subjective nature of reality (in many ways, she out-PKDs her contemporary Phillip K. Dick, here) in direct but subtly calibrated ways, spinning off deep and troubling tangents worthy of their own stories as barest afterthought. Then she uses her sanely-insane apparatus to closely interrogate manifold ethical problems: the individual's responsibility to society, what exactly the greatest good is and can be, whether <i>any</i> individual is even capable of deciding such things. I've noticed other reviews here mentioning the "implausibilty" but this matters not a bit in service of such broad goals as here. Seriously, this is the kind of bold and insane premise that I wish much more sci-fi would dare to tackle. It's the book's best asset.<br /><br />Though, as I said, it's also relentlessly gripping from the start, opening well into its action and then expanding uncontrollably upon the premise without stopping, wasting no time or words, often revealing world-detail elegantly in asides and background description. On the other hand, it's pace, concision, and scope demand a kind of obviousness at odds with some of my deepest-held sensibilities. Though at the time I was so caught up that I barely noticed, and behind the obvious bits, there are subtler shadings of idea flitting about, deepening the picture effectively.<br /><br />I read this right after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/847735.The_Other_Side__Dedalus_European_Classics_" title="The Other Side (Dedalus European Classics) by Alfred Kubin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Other Side</a>, another very different, though also strangely compelling, vision of reality-warping dreamstate. In some ways, by broadcasting its questions more clearly, this is the far less dreamlike and ambiguous of the two, though it probably makes the stronger case for itself, ultimately.<br /><br />As M says "I'd always been more into the idea of Ursula K. Le Guin" (we both read some of the Earthseas decades ago, independently, remember being somewhat bored, know that we should revisit), but this bears out Le Guin's promise quite spectacularly. Recommend me other essentials of hers in this vein, please!<br /><br />Final afterthought: Jonathan Lethem has clearly read this / borrowed concepts and inspirations at least twice.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2788273171979292396" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
